<p>The <b>Quran</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɔːr/ 'or' in 'abhor'">ɔːr</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɑː/ 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-English_pronunciation_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-English_pronunciation-1">[a]</a></sup> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i>kor-<b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AHN</span></span></b></i></a></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">القرآن</span>‎‎ <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">al-Qurʾān</span></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arabic_pronunciation_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arabic_pronunciation-2">[b]</a></sup> literally meaning "the recitation"; also <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a> <b>Qur'an</b> or <b>Koran</b>) is the central <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">religious text</a> of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> believe to be a revelation from <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">الله</span>‎‎, <i><a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup> It is widely regarded as the finest work in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">classical Arabic literature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[5]</a></sup> The Quran is divided into chapters (<i><a href="/wiki/Surah" title="Surah">surah</a></i> in Arabic), which are then divided into verses (<i><a href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">ayah</a></i>).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg/220px-IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg/330px-IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg/440px-IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
11th-century North African Quran in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG/220px-Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG/330px-Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG/440px-Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Quran − in <a href="/wiki/Mashhad" title="Mashhad">Mashhad</a>, Iran − written by <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Muslims believe the Quran was verbally revealed by God to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a> through the angel <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Islam)" title="Holy Spirit (Islam)">Jibril</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambert_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lambert-8">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_.26_Drew_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_.26_Drew-9">[7]</a></sup> gradually over a period of approximately 23 years, beginning on 22 December 609 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup> when Muhammad was 40, and concluding in 632, the year of his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivRlgP338_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-LivRlgP338-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QuranC17V106_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-QuranC17V106-12">[10]</a></sup> Muslims regard the Quran as the most important miracle of Muhammad, a proof of his prophethood,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> and the culmination of a series of divine messages that started with the messages revealed to <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a> and ended with Muhammad. The word "Quran" occurs some 70 times in the text of the Quran, although different names and words are also said to be references to the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler2002_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler2002-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the traditional narrative, several companions of Muhammad served as scribes and were responsible for writing down the revelations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donner-Companion_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donner-Companion-15">[13]</a></sup> Shortly after Muhammad's death, the Quran was compiled by his companions who wrote down and memorized parts of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jecampo_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-jecampo-16">[14]</a></sup> These codices had differences that motivated the Caliph <a href="/wiki/Uthman" title="Uthman">Uthman</a> to establish a standard version now known as Uthman's codex, which is generally considered the archetype of the Quran known today. There are, however, <a href="/wiki/Qira%27at" title="Qira'at">variant readings</a>, with mostly minor differences in meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donner-Companion_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Donner-Companion-15">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran assumes familiarity with major narratives recounted in the <a href="/wiki/Books_of_the_Bible" title="Books of the Bible">Biblical scriptures</a>. It summarizes some, dwells at length on others and, in some cases, presents alternative accounts and interpretations of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sanigosian_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-sanigosian-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> The Quran describes itself as a book of guidance. It sometimes offers detailed accounts of specific historical events, and it often emphasizes the moral significance of an event over its narrative sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> The Quran is used along with the <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i> to interpret <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia"><i>sharia</i> law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> During prayers, the Quran is recited only in Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Someone who has memorized the entire Quran is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">hafiz</a></i>. Some Muslims read Quranic <a href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">ayah</a> (verse) with <a href="/wiki/Elocution" title="Elocution">elocution</a>, which is often called <i><a href="/wiki/Tajwid" title="Tajwid">tajwid</a></i>. During the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>, Muslims typically complete the recitation of the whole Quran during <i><a href="/wiki/Tarawih" title="Tarawih">tarawih</a></i> prayers. In order to extrapolate the meaning of a particular Quranic verse, most Muslims rely on the <i><a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">tafsir</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_meaning">Etymology and meaning</span></h2>
<p>The word <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">qurʼān</span></i> appears about 70 times in the Quran itself, assuming various meanings. It is a <a href="/wiki/Verbal_noun" title="Verbal noun">verbal noun</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Arabic_verbs#Verbal_noun_.28ma.E1.B9.A3dar.29" title="Arabic verbs"><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">maṣdar</span></a></i>) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> verb <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">qaraʼa</span></i> (<span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">قرأ</span>), meaning "he read" or "he recited". The <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> equivalent is (<span lang="syc" xml:lang="syc">ܩܪܝܢܐ</span>) <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Semitic transliteration">qeryānā</span></i>, which refers to "scripture reading" or "lesson".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comprehensive_Aramaic_Lexicon_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Comprehensive_Aramaic_Lexicon-25">[23]</a></sup> While some Western scholars consider the word to be derived from the Syriac, the majority of Muslim authorities hold the origin of the word is <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">qaraʼa</span></i> itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup> Regardless, it had become an Arabic term by Muhammad's lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup> An important meaning of the word is the "act of reciting", as reflected in an early Quranic passage: "It is for Us to collect it and to recite it (<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">qurʼānahu</span>)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>In other verses, the word refers to "an individual passage recited [by Muhammad]". Its <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgical</a> context is seen in a number of passages, for example: <i>"So when</i> <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">al-qurʼān</span> <i>is recited, listen to it and keep silent."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> The word may also assume the meaning of a codified scripture when mentioned with other scriptures such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tawrat" title="Tawrat">Torah</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injil" title="Injil">Gospel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term also has closely related <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonyms</a> that are employed throughout the Quran. Each synonym possesses its own distinct meaning, but its use may converge with that of <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">qurʼān</span></i> in certain contexts. Such terms include <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Kitab" title="Kitab">kitāb</a></span></i> (book); <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">āyah</a></span></i> (sign); and <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar transliteration"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sura" title="Sura">sūrah</a></span></i> (scripture). The latter two terms also denote units of revelation. In the large majority of contexts, usually with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite_article" title="Definite article">definite article</a> (<i>al-</i>), the word is referred to as the "revelation" (<i><a href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">waḥy</a></i>), that which has been "sent down" (<i><a href="/wiki/Tanzil" title="Tanzil">tanzīl</a></i>) at intervals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> Other related words are: <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Dhikr" title="Dhikr">dhikr</a></span></i> (remembrance), used to refer to the Quran in the sense of a reminder and warning, and <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar transliteration">ḥikmah</span></i> (wisdom), sometimes referring to the revelation or part of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran describes itself as "the discernment" (<i>al-furqān</i>), "the mother book" (<i>umm al-kitāb</i>), "the guide" (<i><a href="/wiki/Huda_(name)" title="Huda (name)">huda</a></i>), "the wisdom" (<i><a href="/wiki/Hikmah" title="Hikmah">hikmah</a></i>), "the remembrance" (<i>dhikr</i>) and "the revelation" (<i>tanzīl</i>; something sent down, signifying the descent of an object from a higher place to lower place).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaffer_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jaffer-32">[30]</a></sup> Another term is <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">al-kitāb</span></i> (The Book), though it is also used in the Arabic language for other scriptures, such as the Torah and the Gospels. The adjective of "Quran" has multiple transliterations including "quranic", "koranic", and "qur'anic", or capitalised as "Qur'anic", "Koranic", and "Quranic". The term <i><a href="/wiki/Mus%27haf" title="Mus'haf">mus'haf</a></i> ('written work') is often used to refer to particular Quranic manuscripts but is also used in the Quran to identify earlier revealed books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup> Other transliterations of "Quran" include "al-Coran", "Coran", "Kuran", and "al-Qurʼan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophetic_era">Prophetic era</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">Wahy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cave_Hira.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1976" data-file-width="1721" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Cave_Hira.jpg/150px-Cave_Hira.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Cave_Hira.jpg/225px-Cave_Hira.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Cave_Hira.jpg/300px-Cave_Hira.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cave_Hira.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cave of Hira, location of Muhammad's first revelation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Islamic tradition relates that <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a> received <a href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_first_revelation" title="Muhammad's first revelation">his first revelation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hira" title="Hira">Cave of Hira</a> during one of his isolated retreats to the mountains. Thereafter, he received revelations over a period of 23 years. According to <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i> and Muslim history, after Muhammad <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">immigrated to Medina</a> and formed an independent Muslim community, he ordered many of his <a href="/wiki/Sahabah" title="Sahabah">companions</a> to recite the Quran and to learn and teach the laws, which were revealed daily. It is related that some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quraysh_tribe" title="Quraysh tribe">Quraysh</a> who were taken prisoners at the battle of Badr regained their freedom after they had taught some of the Muslims the simple writing of the time. Thus a group of Muslims gradually became literate. As it was initially spoken, the Quran was recorded on tablets, bones, and the wide, flat ends of date palm fronds. Most suras were in use amongst early Muslims since they are mentioned in numerous sayings by both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Shia</a> sources, relating Muhammad's use of the Quran as a call to Islam, the making of prayer and the manner of recitation. However, the Quran did not exist in book form at the time of Muhammad's death in 632.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tabatabai5_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-tabatabai5-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-watt-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chi_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-chi-36">[34]</a></sup> There is agreement among scholars that Muhammad himself did not write down the revelation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-denffer_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-denffer-37">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surat_al-Fatiha_inscribed_upon_the_shoulder_blade_of_a_camel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2230" data-file-width="1069" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Surat_al-Fatiha_inscribed_upon_the_shoulder_blade_of_a_camel.jpg/110px-Surat_al-Fatiha_inscribed_upon_the_shoulder_blade_of_a_camel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Surat_al-Fatiha_inscribed_upon_the_shoulder_blade_of_a_camel.jpg/165px-Surat_al-Fatiha_inscribed_upon_the_shoulder_blade_of_a_camel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Surat_al-Fatiha_inscribed_upon_the_shoulder_blade_of_a_camel.jpg/220px-Surat_al-Fatiha_inscribed_upon_the_shoulder_blade_of_a_camel.jpg 2x" width="110"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Surat_al-Fatiha_inscribed_upon_the_shoulder_blade_of_a_camel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Quranic verse <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, inscribed on the shoulder blade of a camel with inks</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Sahih al-Bukhari</a></i> narrates Muhammad describing the revelations as, "Sometimes it is (revealed) like the ringing of a bell" and <a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Aisha</a> reported, "I saw the Prophet being inspired Divinely on a very cold day and noticed the sweat dropping from his forehead (as the Inspiration was over)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> Muhammad's first revelation, according to the Quran, was accompanied with a vision. The agent of revelation is mentioned as the "one mighty in power",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> the one who "grew clear to view when he was on the uppermost horizon. Then he drew nigh and came down till he was (distant) two bows' length or even nearer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-watt-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> The Islamic studies scholar Welch states in the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia_of_Islam" title="Encyclopaedia of Islam">Encyclopaedia of Islam</a></i> that he believes the graphic descriptions of Muhammad's condition at these moments may be regarded as genuine, because he was severely disturbed after these revelations. According to Welch, these seizures would have been seen by those around him as convincing evidence for the superhuman origin of Muhammad's inspirations. However, Muhammad's critics accused him of being a possessed man, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clairvoyant" title="Clairvoyant">soothsayer</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Magician_(paranormal)" title="Magician (paranormal)">magician</a> since his experiences were similar to those claimed by such figures well known in <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">ancient Arabia</a>. Welch additionally states that it remains uncertain whether these experiences occurred before or after Muhammad's initial claim of prophethood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iqra.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="550" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Iqra.jpg/210px-Iqra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Iqra.jpg/315px-Iqra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Iqra.jpg/420px-Iqra.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iqra.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Part of <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Alaq" title="Al-Alaq">Al-Alaq</a></i> – 96th <i>sura</i> of the Quran – the first revelation received by Muhammad.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Quran describes Muhammad as <i>"ummi"</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> which is traditionally interpreted as "illiterate," but the meaning is rather more complex. Medieval commentators such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Jarir_al-Tabari" title="Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari">Al-Tabari</a> maintained that the term induced two meanings: first, the inability to read or write in general; second, the inexperience or ignorance of the previous books or scriptures (but they gave priority to the first meaning). Muhammad's illiteracy was taken as a sign of the genuineness of his prophethood. For example, according to <a href="/wiki/Fakhr_al-Din_al-Razi" title="Fakhr al-Din al-Razi">Fakhr al-Din al-Razi</a>, if Muhammad had mastered writing and reading he possibly would have been suspected of having studied the books of the ancestors. Some scholars such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Montgomery_Watt" title="William Montgomery Watt">Watt</a> prefer the second meaning of <i>"ummi"</i> – they take it to indicate unfamiliarity with earlier sacred texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-watt-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compilation">Compilation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2048" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg/220px-Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg/330px-Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg/440px-Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript" title="Birmingham Quran manuscript">Birmingham Quran manuscript</a> dated among the oldest in the world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Quran" title="History of the Quran">History of the Quran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sana%27a_manuscript" title="Sana'a manuscript">Sana'a manuscript</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript" title="Birmingham Quran manuscript">Birmingham Quran manuscript</a></div>
<p>In the year 632, after the demise of Muhammad a number of his companions who knew the Quran by heart were killed in a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yamama" title="Battle of Yamama">battle</a> by <a href="/wiki/Musaylimah" title="Musaylimah">Musaylimah</a>, the first caliph <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> (d. 634) decided to collect the book in one volume so that it could be preserved. <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Thabit" title="Zayd ibn Thabit">Zayd ibn Thabit</a> (d. 655) was the person to collect the Quran since "he used to write the Divine Inspiration for Allah's Apostle". Thus, a group of scribes, most importantly Zayd, collected the verses and produced a hand-written manuscript of the complete book. The manuscript according to Zayd remained with Abu Bakr until he died. Zayd's reaction to the task and the difficulties in collecting the Quranic material from parchments, palm-leaf stalks, thin stones and from men who knew it by heart is recorded in earlier narratives. After Abu Bakr, <a href="/wiki/Hafsa_bint_Umar" title="Hafsa bint Umar">Hafsa bint Umar</a>, Muhammad's widow, was entrusted with the manuscript. In about 650, the third Caliph <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_Affan" title="Uthman ibn Affan">Uthman ibn Affan</a> (d. 656) began noticing slight differences in pronunciation of the Quran as Islam expanded beyond the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, and North Africa. In order to preserve the sanctity of the text, he ordered a committee headed by Zayd to use Abu Bakr's copy and prepare a standard copy of the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tabatabai5_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-tabatabai5-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sbukhari1_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-sbukhari1-44">[42]</a></sup> Thus, within 20 years of Muhammad's death, the Quran was committed to written form. That text became the model from which copies were made and promulgated throughout the urban centers of the Muslim world, and other versions are believed to have been destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tabatabai5_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-tabatabai5-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> The present form of the Quran text is accepted by Muslim scholars to be the original version compiled by Abu Bakr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-watt-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chi_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-chi-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a> (d. 661) compiled a complete version of the Quran shortly after Muhammad's death. The order of this text differed from that gathered later during Uthman's era in that this version had been collected in chronological order. Despite this, he made no objection against the standardized Quran and accepted the Quran in circulation. Other personal copies of the Quran might have existed including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd_Allah_ibn_Mas%27ud" title="Abd Allah ibn Mas'ud">Ibn Mas'ud</a>'s and <a href="/wiki/Ubay_ibn_Ka%27b" title="Ubay ibn Ka'b">Ubay ibn Ka'b</a>'s codex, none of which exist today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tabatabai5_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-tabatabai5-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran most likely existed in scattered written form during Muhammad's lifetime. Several sources indicate that during Muhammad's lifetime a large number of his companions had memorized the revelations. Early commentaries and Islamic historical sources support the above-mentioned understanding of the Quran's early development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jecampo_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-jecampo-16">[14]</a></sup> The Quran in its present form is generally considered by academic scholars to record the words spoken by Muhammad because the search for variants has not yielded any differences of great significance.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> professor <a href="/wiki/Fred_Donner" title="Fred Donner">Fred Donner</a> states that "...there was a very early attempt to establish a uniform consonantal text of the Qurʾān from what was probably a wider and more varied group of related texts in early transmission. [...] After the creation of this standardized canonical text, earlier authoritative texts were suppressed, and all extant manuscripts—despite their numerous variants—seem to date to a time after this standard consonantal text was established."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> Although most variant readings of the text of the Quran have ceased to be transmitted, some still are. There has been no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_text" title="Critical text">critical text</a> produced on which a scholarly reconstruction of the Quranic text could be based.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gilliot2006p52_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-gilliot2006p52-52">[50]</a></sup> Historically, controversy over the Quran's content has rarely become an issue, although debates continue on the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1972, in a mosque in the city of <a href="/wiki/Sana%27a" title="Sana'a">Sana'a</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, manuscripts were discovered that were later proved to be the most ancient Quranic text known to exist at the time. The <a href="/wiki/Sana%27a_manuscript" title="Sana'a manuscript">Sana'a manuscripts</a> contain <a href="/wiki/Palimpsest" title="Palimpsest">palimpsests</a>, a manuscript page from which the text has been washed off to make the parchment reusable again—a practice which was common in ancient times due to scarcity of writing material. However, the faint washed-off underlying text (<i>scriptio inferior</i>) is still barely visible and believed to be "pre-Uthmanic" Quranic content, while the text written on top (<i>scriptio superior</i>) is believed to belong to Uthmanic time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jqs1_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-jqs1-55">[53]</a></sup> Studies using <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dating</a> indicate that the parchments are dated to the period before 671 AD with a 99 percent probability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergmann_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-bergmann-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadeghi_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-sadeghi-57">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript" title="Birmingham Quran manuscript">fragments of a very early Quran</a>, dating back to 1370 years ago, were discovered in the library of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Birmingham" title="University of Birmingham">University of Birmingham</a>, England. According to the tests carried out by Oxford University Radiocarbon Accelerator Unit, "with a probability of more than 95%, the parchment was from between 568 and 645". The manuscript is written in <a href="/wiki/Hijazi_script" title="Hijazi script">Hijazi script</a>, an early form of written Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oldest_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-oldest-59">[57]</a></sup> This is possibly the earliest extant exemplar of the Quran, but as the tests allow a range of possible dates, it cannot be said with certainty which of the existing versions is the oldest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oldest_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-oldest-59">[57]</a></sup> Saudi scholar Saud al-Sarhan has expressed doubt over the age of the fragments as they contain dots and chapter separators that are believed to have originated later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup></p>

<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">

<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Islam" title="Template:Islam"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>

</table>
<p>Muslims believe the Quran to be the book of divine guidance revealed from God to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a> through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Islam)" title="Holy Spirit (Islam)">angel Gabriel</a> over a period of 23 years and view the Quran as God's final revelation to humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivRlgP338_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-LivRlgP338-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-61">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">Revelation</a> in Islamic and Quranic contexts means the act of God addressing an individual, conveying a message for a greater number of recipients. The process by which the divine message comes to the heart of a messenger of God is <i><a href="/wiki/Tanzil" title="Tanzil">tanzil</a></i> (to send down) or <i>nuzūl</i> (to come down). As the Quran says, "With the truth we (God) have sent it down and with the truth it has come down."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran frequently asserts in its text that it is divinely ordained. Some verses in the Quran seem to imply that even those who do not speak Arabic would understand the Quran if it were recited to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jenssen2001_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-jenssen2001-63">[61]</a></sup> The Quran refers to a written pre-text, "the preserved tablet", that records God's speech even before it was sent down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsonn_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsonn-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>The issue of whether the Quran is eternal or created became a theological debate (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Createdness_(Qu%27ran)" title="Createdness (Qu'ran)">Quran's createdness</a>) in the ninth century. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%27tazila" title="Mu'tazila">Mu'tazilas</a>, an Islamic school of theology based on reason and rational thought, held that the Quran was created while the most widespread varieties of Muslim theologians considered the Quran to be co-eternal with God and therefore uncreated. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> philosophers view the question as artificial or wrongly framed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Muslims believe that the present wording of the Quran corresponds to that revealed to Muhammad, and according to their interpretation of Quran <span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/15:9" rel="nofollow">15:9</a></span>, it is protected from corruption ("Indeed, it is We who sent down the Quran and indeed, We will be its guardian.").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup> Muslims consider the Quran to be a guide, a sign of the prophethood of Muhammad and the truth of the religion.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inimitability">Inimitability</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/I%27jaz" title="I'jaz">I'jaz</a></div>
<p>Inimitability of the Quran (or "<i>I'jaz</i>") is the belief that no human speech can match the Quran in its content and form. The Quran is considered an inimitable miracle by Muslims, effective until the Day of Resurrection—and, thereby, the central proof granted to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> in authentication of his prophetic status. The concept of inimitability originates in the Quran where in five different verses opponents are challenged to produce something like the Quran: "If men and sprites banded together to produce the like of this Quran they would never produce its like not though they backed one another."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup> So the suggestion is that if there are doubts concerning the divine authorship of the Quran, come forward and create something like it. From the ninth century, numerous works appeared which studied the Quran and examined its style and content. Medieval Muslim scholars including <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Qahir_al-Jurjani" title="Abd al-Qahir al-Jurjani">al-Jurjani</a> (d. 1078) and <a href="/wiki/Al-Baqillani" title="Al-Baqillani">al-Baqillani</a> (d. 1013) have written treatises on the subject, discussed its various aspects, and used linguistic approaches to study the Quran. Others argue that the Quran contains noble ideas, has inner meanings, maintained its freshness through the ages and has caused great transformations in individual level and in the history. Some scholars state that the Quran contains scientific information that agrees with modern science. The doctrine of miraculousness of the Quran is further emphasized by Muhammad's illiteracy since the unlettered prophet could not have been suspected of composing the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sophia_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-sophia-69">[67]</a></sup> Muslims argue it is not possible for a human to produce a book like the Quran, as the Quran itself maintains.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_worship">In worship</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Salah" title="Salah">Salah</a></div>
<p>The first sura of the Quran is repeated in daily prayers and in other occasions. This sura, which consists of seven verses, is the most often recited sura of the Quran:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>Praised be God, Lord of the Universe, the Beneficent, the Merciful and Master of the Day of Judgment, You alone We do worship and from You alone we do seek assistance, guide us to the right path, the path of those to whom You have granted blessings, those who are neither subject to Your anger nor have gone astray."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Other sections of the Quran of choice are also read in daily prayers.</p>
<p>Respect for the written text of the Quran is an important element of religious faith by many Muslims, and the Quran is treated with reverence. Based on tradition and a literal interpretation of Quran <span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/56:79" rel="nofollow">56:79</a></span> ("none shall touch but those who are clean"), some Muslims believe that they must perform a ritual cleansing with water before touching a copy of the Quran, although this view is not universal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup> Worn-out copies of the Quran are wrapped in a cloth and stored indefinitely in a safe place, buried in a mosque or a Muslim cemetery, or burned and the ashes buried or scattered over water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Islam, most intellectual disciplines, including Islamic theology, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">mysticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">jurisprudence</a>, have been concerned with the Quran or have their foundation in its teachings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup> Muslims believe that the preaching or reading of the Quran is rewarded with divine rewards variously called <i>ajr</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sawab" title="Sawab">thawab</a></i> or <i>hasanat</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Islamic_art">In Islamic art</span></h3>
<p>The Quran also inspired <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic arts</a> and specifically the so-called Quranic arts of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">calligraphy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_illumination" title="Ottoman illumination">illumination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup> The Quran is never decorated with figurative images, but many Qurans have been highly decorated with decorative patterns in the margins of the page, or between the lines or at the start of suras. Islamic verses appear in many other media, on buildings and on objects of all sizes, such as <a href="/wiki/Mosque_lamp" title="Mosque lamp">mosque lamps</a>, metal work, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_pottery" title="Islamic pottery">pottery</a> and single pages of calligraphy for <a href="/wiki/Muraqqa" title="Muraqqa">muraqqas</a> or albums.</p>
