<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Text_and_arrangement">Text and arrangement</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sura" title="Sura">Sura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">Ayah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FirstSurahKoran_(fragment).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="444" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/230px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/345px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FirstSurahKoran_(fragment).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First sura of the Quran, <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Fatiha" title="Al-Fatiha">Al-Fatiha</a></i>, consisting of seven verses.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Quran consists of 114 chapters of varying lengths, each known as a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sura" title="Sura">sura</a></i>. Suras are classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meccan_sura" title="Meccan sura">Meccan</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medinan_sura" title="Medinan sura">Medinan</a>, depending on whether the verses were revealed before or after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">migration</a> of Muhammad to the city of Medina. However, a sura classified as Medinan may contain Meccan verses in it and vice versa. Sura titles are derived from a name or quality discussed in the text, or from the first letters or words of the sura. Suras are arranged roughly in order of decreasing size. The sura arrangement is thus not connected to the sequence of revelation. Each sura except the ninth starts with the <i><a href="/wiki/Basmala" title="Basmala">Bismillah</a></i> (<span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم</span>), an Arabic phrase meaning "In the name of God". There are, however, still 114 occurrences of the <i>Bismillah</i> in the Quran, due to its presence in <span class="plainlinks">Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/27:30" rel="nofollow">27:30</a></span> as the opening of <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a>'s letter to the <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Sheba" title="Queen of Sheba">Queen of Sheba</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surah95-Tin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="541" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Surah95-Tin.png/230px-Surah95-Tin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Surah95-Tin.png/345px-Surah95-Tin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Surah95-Tin.png/460px-Surah95-Tin.png 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Surah95-Tin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/At-Tin" title="At-Tin">At-Tin</a></i> (the fig), 95th sura of the Quran.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Each sura consists of several verses, known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayat" title="Ayat">ayat</a></i>, which originally means a "sign" or "evidence" sent by God. The number of verses differs from sura to sura. An individual verse may be just a few letters or several lines. The total number of verses in the Quran is 6,236; however, the number varies if the <i>bismillahs</i> are counted separately.</p>
<p>In addition to and independent of the division into suras, there are various ways of dividing the Quran into parts of approximately equal length for convenience in reading. The 30 <i><a href="/wiki/Juz%27" title="Juz'">juz'</a></i> (plural <i>ajzāʼ</i>) can be used to read through the entire Quran in a month. Some of these parts are known by names—which are the first few words by which the <i>juzʼ</i> starts. A <i>juz'</i> is sometimes further divided into two <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hizb" title="Hizb">ḥizb</a></i> (plural <i>aḥzāb</i>), and each <i>hizb</i> subdivided into four <i>rubʻ al-ahzab</i>. The Quran is also divided into seven approximately equal parts, <i><a href="/wiki/Manzil" title="Manzil">manzil</a></i> (plural <i>manāzil</i>), for it to be recited in a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>A different structure is provided by semantical units resembling paragraphs and comprising roughly ten <i>ayat</i> each. Such a section is called a <a href="/wiki/Ruku" title="Ruku">rukū`</a>.</p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Muqatta%CA%BFat" title="Muqattaʿat">Muqattaʿat</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">حروف مقطعات</span> ‎‎ <i>ḥurūf muqaṭṭaʿāt</i> "disjoined letters" or "disconnected letters";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> also "mysterious letters") are combinations of between one and five <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic letters</a> figuring at the beginning of 29 out of the 114 surahs (chapters) of the [Quran just after the basmala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Massey2005_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Massey2005-75">[73]</a></sup> The letters are also known as <i>fawātih</i> (<span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">فواتح</span>) or "openers" as they form the opening verse of their respective suras . Four surahs are named for their <i>muqatta'at</i>, <a href="/wiki/Ta-Ha" title="Ta-Ha"><i>Ṭāʾ-Hāʾ</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Ya_Sin" title="Ya Sin"><i>Yāʾ-Sīn</i></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sad_(sura)" title="Sad (sura)"><i>Ṣād</i></a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qaf_(sura)" title="Qaf (sura)"><i>Qāf</i></a>. The original significance of the letters is unknown. <a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">Tafsir</a> (<a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a>) has interpreted them as abbreviations for either <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">names or qualities of God</a> or for the names or content of the respective surahs.</p>
<p>According to one estimate the Quran consists of 77,430 words, 18,994 unique words, 12,183 <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">stems</a>, 3,382 <a href="/wiki/Lemma_(morphology)" title="Lemma (morphology)">lemmas</a> and 1,685 <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">roots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contents">Contents</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God in Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">Islamic eschatology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">Prophets in Islam</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quran_and_science" title="Quran and science">Quran and science</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legends_and_the_Quran" title="Legends and the Quran">Legends and the Quran</a></div>
<p>The Quranic content is concerned with basic Islamic beliefs including the existence of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">resurrection</a>. Narratives of the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">prophets</a>, ethical and legal subjects, historical events of Muhammad's time, charity and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">prayer</a> also appear in the Quran. The Quranic verses contain general exhortations regarding right and wrong and historical events are related to outline general moral lessons. Verses pertaining to natural phenomena have been interpreted by Muslims as an indication of the authenticity of the Quranic message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saeed_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-saeed-77">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monotheism">Monotheism</span></h3>
<p>The central theme of the Quran is <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a>. God is depicted as living, eternal, omniscient and omnipotent (see, e.g., <span class="plainlinks">Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/2:20" rel="nofollow">2:20</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/2:29" rel="nofollow">2:29</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/2:255" rel="nofollow">2:255</a></span>). God's omnipotence appears above all in his power to create. He is the creator of everything, of the heavens and the earth and what is between them (see, e.g., <span class="plainlinks">Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/13:16" rel="nofollow">13:16</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/50:38" rel="nofollow">50:38</a>, etc.).</span> All human beings are equal in their utter dependence upon God,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (December 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and their well-being depends upon their acknowledging that fact and living accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-watt-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saeed_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-saeed-77">[75]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quran_rzabasi4.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1811" data-file-width="4197" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Quran_rzabasi4.JPG/220px-Quran_rzabasi4.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Quran_rzabasi4.JPG/330px-Quran_rzabasi4.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Quran_rzabasi4.JPG/440px-Quran_rzabasi4.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quran_rzabasi4.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Written in the 12th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Quran uses <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_argument" title="Cosmological argument">cosmological</a> and contingency arguments in various verses without referring to the terms to prove the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>. Therefore, the universe is originated and needs an originator, and whatever exists must have a sufficient cause for its existence. Besides, the design of the universe is frequently referred to as a point of contemplation: "It is He who has created seven heavens in harmony. You cannot see any fault in God's creation; then look again: Can you see any flaw?"<span class="plainlinks"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eschatology">Eschatology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">Islamic eschatology</a></div>
<p>The doctrine of the last day and <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschatology</a> (the final fate of the universe) may be reckoned as the second great doctrine of the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-watt-35">[33]</a></sup> It is estimated that approximately one-third of the Quran is eschatological, dealing with the afterlife in the next world and with the day of judgment at the end of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-45">[43]</a></sup> There is a reference to the afterlife on most pages of the Quran and belief in the afterlife is often referred to in conjunction with belief in God as in the common expression: "Believe in God and the last day".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haleem_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-haleem-79">[77]</a></sup> A number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sura" title="Sura">suras</a> such as 44, 56, 75, 78, 81 and 101 are directly related to the afterlife and its preparations. Some suras indicate the closeness of the event and warn people to be prepared for the imminent day. For instance, the first verses of Sura 22, which deal with the mighty earthquake and the situations of people on that day, represent this style of divine address: "O People! Be respectful to your Lord. The earthquake of the Hour is a mighty thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran is often vivid in its depiction of what will happen at the end time. Watt describes the Quranic view of End Time:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_35-6"><a href="#cite_note-watt-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"The climax of history, when the present world comes to an end, is referred to in various ways. It is 'the Day of Judgment,' 'the Last Day,' 'the Day of Resurrection,' or simply 'the Hour.' Less frequently it is 'the Day of Distinction' (when the good are separated from the evil), 'the Day of the Gathering' (of men to the presence of God) or 'the Day of the Meeting' (of men with God). The Hour comes suddenly. It is heralded by a shout, by a thunderclap, or by the blast of a trumpet. A cosmic upheaval then takes place. The mountains dissolve into dust, the seas boil up, the sun is darkened, the stars fall and the sky is rolled up. God appears as Judge, but his presence is hinted at rather than described. [...] The central interest, of course, is in the gathering of all mankind before the Judge. Human beings of all ages, restored to life, join the throng. To the scoffing objection of the unbelievers that former generations had been dead a long time and were now dust and mouldering bones, the reply is that God is nevertheless able to restore them to life."</dd>
</dl>
<p>The Quran does not assert a natural <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">immortality</a> of the human <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>, since man's existence is dependent on the will of God: when he wills, he causes man to die; and when he wills, he raises him to life again in a bodily <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rcmartin_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-rcmartin-80">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophets">Prophets</span></h3>
<p>According to the Quran, God communicated with man and made his will known through signs and revelations. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">Prophets</a>, or 'Messengers of God', received revelations and delivered them to humanity. The message has been identical and for all humankind. "Nothing is said to you that was not said to the messengers before you, that your lord has at his Command forgiveness as well as a most Grievous Penalty."<span class="plainlinks"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup></span> The revelation does not come directly from God to the prophets. Angels acting as God's messengers deliver the divine revelation to them. This comes out in <span class="plainlinks">Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/42:51" rel="nofollow">42:51</a></span>, in which it is stated: "It is not for any mortal that God should speak to them, except by revelation, or from behind a veil, or by sending a messenger to reveal by his permission whatsoever He will."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rcmartin_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-rcmartin-80">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethico-religious_concepts">Ethico-religious concepts</span></h3>
<p>Belief is a fundamental aspect of morality in the Quran, and scholars have tried to determine the semantic contents of "belief" and "believer" in the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toshihiko_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-toshihiko-82">[80]</a></sup> The ethico-legal concepts and exhortations dealing with righteous conduct are linked to a profound awareness of God, thereby emphasizing the importance of faith, accountability, and the belief in each human's ultimate encounter with God. People are invited to perform acts of charity, especially for the needy. Believers who "spend of their wealth by night and by day, in secret and in public" are promised that they "shall have their reward with their Lord; on them shall be no fear, nor shall they grieve".<span class="plainlinks"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup></span> It also affirms family life by legislating on matters of marriage, divorce, and inheritance. A number of practices, such as usury and gambling, are prohibited. The Quran is one of the fundamental sources of Islamic law (<i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a></i>). Some formal religious practices receive significant attention in the Quran including the formal prayers (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">salat</a></i>) and fasting in the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>. As for the manner in which the prayer is to be conducted, the Quran refers to prostration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jecampo_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-jecampo-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rcmartin_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-rcmartin-80">[78]</a></sup> The term for charity, <i><a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">zakat</a></i>, literally means purification. Charity, according to the Quran, is a means of self-purification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsonn_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-tsonn-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Encouragement_for_the_sciences">Encouragement for the sciences</span></h3>
<p>The astrophysicist <a href="/wiki/Nidhal_Guessoum" title="Nidhal Guessoum">Nidhal Guessoum</a> while being highly critical of pseudo-scientific claims made about the Quran, has highlighted the encouragement for sciences that the Quran provides by developing "the concept of knowledge.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> He writes: "The Qur'an draws attention to the danger of conjecturing without evidence (<i>And follow not that of which you have not the (certain) knowledge of...</i> 17:36) and in several different verses asks Muslims to require proofs (<i>Say: Bring your proof if you are truthful</i> 2:111), both in matters of theological belief and in natural science." Guessoum cites Ghaleb Hasan on the definition of "proof" according the Quran being "clear and strong... convincing evidence or argument." Also, such a proof cannot rely on an <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_authority" title="Argument from authority">argument from authority</a>, citing verse 5:104. Lastly, both assertions and rejections require a proof, according to verse 4:174.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ismail_al-Faruqi" title="Ismail al-Faruqi">Ismail al-Faruqi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taha_Jabir_Alalwani" title="Taha Jabir Alalwani">Taha Jabir Alalwani</a> are of the view that any reawakening of the Muslim civilization must start with the Quran; however, the biggest obstacle on this route is the "centuries old heritage of tafseer (exegesis) and other classical disciplines" which inhibit a "universal, epidemiological and systematic conception" of the Quran's message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Iqbal" title="Muhammad Iqbal">Muhammad Iqbal</a>, considered the Quran's methodology and epistemology to be empirical and rational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>It's generally accepted<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> that there are around 750 verses<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (December 2016)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> in the Quran dealing with natural phenomena. In many of these verses the study of nature is <i>"encouraged and highly recommended,"</i> and historical Islamic scientists like <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Battani" title="Al-Battani">Al-Battani</a> derived their inspiration from verses of the Quran.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs additional references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">additional citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Hashim_Kamali" title="Mohammad Hashim Kamali">Mohammad Hashim Kamali</a> has the stated that "scientific observation, experimental knowledge and rationality" are the primary tools with which humanity can achieve the goals laid out for it in the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ziauddin_Sardar" title="Ziauddin Sardar">Ziauddin Sardar</a> built a case for Muslims having developed the foundations of modern science, by highlighting the repeated calls of the Quran to observe and reflect upon natural phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> "The 'scientific method,' as it is understood today, was first developed by Muslim scientists" like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Ibn al-Haytham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a>, along with numerous other Muslim scientists.</p>
<p>The physicist <a href="/wiki/Abdus_Salam" title="Abdus Salam">Abdus Salam</a>, in his Nobel Prize banquet address, quoted a well known verse from the Quran (67:3–4) and then stated: "This in effect is the faith of all physicists: the deeper we seek, the more is our wonder excited, the more is the dazzlement of our gaze".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> One of Salam's core beliefs was that there is no contradiction between Islam and the discoveries that science allows humanity to make about nature and the universe. Salam also held the opinion that the Quran and the Islamic spirit of study and rational reflection was the source of extraordinary civilizational development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> Salam highlights, in particular, the work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Ibn al-Haytham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> as the pioneers of empiricism who introduced the experimental approach, breaking way from Aristotle's influence, and thus giving birth to modern science. Salam was also careful to differentiate between metaphysics and physics, and advised against empirically probing certain matters on which "physics is silent and will remain so," such as the doctrine of "creation from nothing" which in Salam's view is outside the limits of science and thus "gives way" to religious considerations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_style">Literary style</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Touba3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Touba3.jpg/200px-Touba3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Touba3.jpg/300px-Touba3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Touba3.jpg/400px-Touba3.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Touba3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Boys studying Quran, <a href="/wiki/Touba,_Senegal" title="Touba, Senegal">Touba, Senegal</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Quran's message is conveyed with various literary structures and devices. In the original Arabic, the suras and verses employ <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theme_(literature)" title="Theme (literature)">thematic</a> structures that assist the audience's efforts to recall the message of the text. Muslims<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> assert (according to the Quran itself) that the Quranic content and style is inimitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Issa_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Issa-94">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The language of the Quran has been described as "rhymed prose" as it partakes of both poetry and prose; however, this description runs the risk of failing to convey the rhythmic quality of Quranic language, which is more poetic in some parts and more prose-like in others. Rhyme, while found throughout the Quran, is conspicuous in many of the earlier Meccan suras, in which relatively short verses throw the rhyming words into prominence. The effectiveness of such a form is evident for instance in Sura 81, and there can be no doubt that these passages impressed the conscience of the hearers. Frequently a change of rhyme from one set of verses to another signals a change in the subject of discussion. Later sections also preserve this form but the style is more expository.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quranic text seems to have no beginning, middle, or end, its nonlinear structure being akin to a web or net.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup> The textual arrangement is sometimes considered to exhibit lack of continuity, absence of any chronological or thematic order and repetitiousness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blomm_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-blomm-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pepys_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-pepys-97">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Sells" title="Michael Sells">Michael Sells</a>, citing the work of the critic <a href="/wiki/Norman_O._Brown" title="Norman O. Brown">Norman O. Brown</a>, acknowledges Brown's observation that the seeming disorganization of Quranic literary expression – its scattered or fragmented mode of composition in Sells's phrase – is in fact a literary device capable of delivering profound effects as if the intensity of the prophetic message were shattering the vehicle of human language in which it was being communicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApproachQuran_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApproachQuran-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup> Sells also addresses the much-discussed repetitiveness of the Quran, seeing this, too, as a literary device.</p>
<p>A text is <a href="/wiki/Self-reference" title="Self-reference">self-referential</a> when it speaks about itself and makes reference to itself. According to Stefan Wild, the Quran demonstrates this <a href="/wiki/Metatextuality" title="Metatextuality">metatextuality</a> by explaining, classifying, interpreting and justifying the words to be transmitted. Self-referentiality is evident in those passages where the Quran refers to itself as revelation (<i>tanzil</i>), remembrance (<i>dhikr</i>), news (<i>naba'</i>), criterion (<i>furqan</i>) in a self-designating manner (explicitly asserting its Divinity, "And this is a blessed Remembrance that We have sent down; so are you now denying it?"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup> or in the frequent appearance of the "Say" tags, when Muhammad is commanded to speak (e.g., "Say: 'God's guidance is the true guidance'", "Say: 'Would you then dispute with us concerning God?'"). According to Wild the Quran is highly self-referential. The feature is more evident in early Meccan suras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretation">Interpretation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">Tafsir</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tapurian_Qur%27an_(Al-Kusar).PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="497" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG/280px-Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG/420px-Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tapurian_Qur%27an_(Al-Kusar).PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An early interpretation of Sura 108 of the Quran</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Quran has sparked a huge body of commentary and explication (<i>tafsir</i>), aimed at explaining the "meanings of the Quranic verses, clarifying their import and finding out their significance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-102">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tafsir is one of the earliest academic activities of Muslims. According to the Quran, Muhammad was the first person who described the meanings of verses for early Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> Other early exegetes included a few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Companions_of_Muhammad" title="Companions of Muhammad">Companions of Muhammad</a>, like ʻ<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a>, ʻ<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdullah_ibn_Abbas" title="Abdullah ibn Abbas">Abdullah ibn Abbas</a>, ʻ<a href="/wiki/Abdullah_ibn_Umar" title="Abdullah ibn Umar">Abdullah ibn Umar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ubayy_ibn_Kab" title="Ubayy ibn Kab">Ubayy ibn Kaʻb</a>. Exegesis in those days was confined to the explanation of literary aspects of the verse, the background of its revelation and, occasionally, interpretation of one verse with the help of the other. If the verse was about a historical event, then sometimes a few traditions (<i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i>) of Muhammad were narrated to make its meaning clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-102">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because the Quran is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">classical Arabic</a>, many of the later converts to Islam (mostly non-Arabs) did not always understand the Quranic Arabic, they did not catch allusions that were clear to early Muslims fluent in Arabic and they were concerned with reconciling apparent conflict of themes in the Quran. Commentators erudite in Arabic explained the allusions, and perhaps most importantly, explained which Quranic verses had been revealed early in Muhammad's prophetic career, as being appropriate to the very earliest Muslim community, and which had been revealed later, canceling out or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naskh_(exegesis)" title="Naskh (exegesis)">abrogating</a>" (<i>nāsikh</i>) the earlier text (<i>mansūkh</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup> Other scholars, however, maintain that no abrogation has taken place in the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya_Muslim_Community" title="Ahmadiyya Muslim Community">Ahmadiyya Muslim Community</a> has published a ten-volume Urdu commentary on the Quran, with the name <i>Tafseer e Kabir</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Esoteric_interpretation">Esoteric interpretation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Esoteric_interpretation_of_the_Quran" title="Esoteric interpretation of the Quran">Esoteric interpretation of the Quran</a></div>
<p>Esoteric or <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> interpretation attempts to unveil the inner meanings of the Quran. Sufism moves beyond the apparent (<i>zahir</i>) point of the verses and instead relates Quranic verses to the inner or esoteric (<i>batin</i>) and metaphysical dimensions of consciousness and existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alangodlas_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-alangodlas-108">[106]</a></sup> According to Sands, esoteric interpretations are more suggestive than declarative, they are allusions (<i>isharat</i>) rather than explanations (<i>tafsir</i>). They indicate possibilities as much as they demonstrate the insights of each writer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kristin_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-kristin-109">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sufi interpretation, according to Annabel Keeler, also exemplifies the use of the theme of love, as for instance can be seen in Qushayri's interpretation of the Quran. <span class="plainlinks">Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/7:143" rel="nofollow">7:143</a></span> says:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>when Moses came at the time we appointed, and his Lord spoke to him, he said, 'My Lord, show yourself to me! Let me see you!' He said, 'you shall not see me but look at that mountain, if it remains standing firm you will see me.' When his Lord revealed Himself to the mountain, He made it crumble. Moses fell down unconscious. When he recovered, he said, 'Glory be to you! I repent to you! I am the first to believe!'</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Moses, in 7:143, comes the way of those who are in love, he asks for a vision but his desire is denied, he is made to suffer by being commanded to look at other than the Beloved while the mountain is able to see God. The mountain crumbles and Moses faints at the sight of God's manifestation upon the mountain. In Qushayri's words, Moses came like thousands of men who traveled great distances, and there was nothing left to Moses of Moses. In that state of annihilation from himself, Moses was granted the unveiling of the realities. From the Sufi point of view, God is the always the beloved and the wayfarer's longing and suffering lead to realization of the truths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keeler_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-keeler-110">[108]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1875" data-file-width="2813" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/220px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/330px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/440px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Men reading the Quran</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Husayn_Tabatabaei" title="Muhammad Husayn Tabatabaei">Muhammad Husayn Tabatabaei</a> says that according to the popular explanation among the later exegetes, <i>ta'wil</i> indicates the particular meaning a verse is directed towards. The meaning of revelation (<i><a href="/wiki/Tanzil" title="Tanzil">tanzil</a></i>), as opposed to <i>ta'wil</i>, is clear in its accordance to the obvious meaning of the words as they were revealed. But this explanation has become so widespread that, at present, it has become the primary meaning of <i>ta'wil</i>, which originally meant "to return" or "the returning place". In Tabatabaei's view, what has been rightly called <i>ta'wil</i>, or hermeneutic interpretation of the Quran, is not concerned simply with the denotation of words. Rather, it is concerned with certain truths and realities that transcend the comprehension of the common run of men; yet it is from these truths and realities that the principles of doctrine and the practical injunctions of the Quran issue forth. Interpretation is not the meaning of the verse—rather it transpires through that meaning, in a special sort of transpiration. There is a spiritual reality—which is the main objective of ordaining a law, or the basic aim in describing a divine attribute—and then there is an actual significance that a Quranic story refers to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ta.27wil_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ta.27wil-111">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Meaning_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Meaning-112">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Shia beliefs, those who are firmly rooted in knowledge like Muhammad and the imams know the secrets of the Quran. According to Tabatabaei, the statement "none knows its interpretation except God" remains valid, without any opposing or qualifying clause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> Therefore, so far as this verse is concerned, the knowledge of the Quran's interpretation is reserved for God. But Tabatabaei uses other verses and concludes that those who are purified by God know the interpretation of the Quran to a certain extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Meaning_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Meaning-112">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Husayn_Tabatabaei" title="Muhammad Husayn Tabatabaei">Tabatabaei</a>, there are acceptable and unacceptable esoteric interpretations. Acceptable <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta%27wil" title="Ta'wil">ta'wil</a></i> refers to the meaning of a verse beyond its literal meaning; rather the implicit meaning, which ultimately is known only to <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> and can't be comprehended directly through human thought alone. The verses in question here refer to the human qualities of coming, going, sitting, satisfaction, anger and sorrow, which are apparently attributed to <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>. Unacceptable <i>ta'wil</i> is where one "transfers" the apparent meaning of a verse to a different meaning by means of a proof; this method is not without obvious inconsistencies. Although this unacceptable <i>ta'wil</i> has gained considerable acceptance, it is incorrect and cannot be applied to the Quranic verses. The correct interpretation is that reality a verse refers to. It is found in all verses, the decisive and the ambiguous alike; it is not a sort of a meaning of the word; it is a fact that is too sublime for words. God has dressed them with words to bring them a bit nearer to our minds; in this respect they are like proverbs that are used to create a picture in the mind, and thus help the hearer to clearly grasp the intended idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Meaning_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Meaning-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabatabaee_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tabatabaee-114">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Sufi_commentaries">History of Sufi commentaries</span></h4>
<p>One of the notable authors of esoteric interpretation prior to the 12th century is Sulami (d. 1021) without whose work the majority of very early Sufi commentaries would not have been preserved. Sulami's major commentary is a book named <i>haqaiq al-tafsir</i> ("Truths of Exegesis") which is a compilation of commentaries of earlier Sufis. From the 11th century onwards several other works appear, including commentaries by Qushayri (d. 1074), Daylami (d. 1193), Shirazi (d. 1209) and Suhrawardi (d. 1234). These works include material from Sulami's books plus the author's contributions. Many works are written in Persian such as the works of Maybudi (d. 1135) <i>kash al-asrar</i> ("the unveiling of the secrets").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alangodlas_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-alangodlas-108">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a> (d. 1273) wrote a vast amount of mystical poetry in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Masnavi" title="Masnavi">Mathnawi</a></i>. Rumi makes heavy use of the Quran in his poetry, a feature that is sometimes omitted in translations of Rumi's work. A large number of Quranic passages can be found in <i>Mathnawi</i>, which some consider a kind of Sufi interpretation of the Quran. Rumi's book is not exceptional for containing citations from and elaboration on the Quran, however, Rumi does mention Quran more frequently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmojaddedi_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-jmojaddedi-115">[113]</a></sup> Simnani (d. 1336) wrote two influential works of esoteric exegesis on the Quran. He reconciled notions of God's manifestation through and in the physical world with the sentiments of Sunni Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jelias_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-jelias-116">[114]</a></sup> Comprehensive Sufi commentaries appear in the 18th century such as the work of Ismail Hakki Bursevi (d. 1725). His work <i>ruh al-Bayan</i> (the Spirit of Elucidation) is a voluminous exegesis. Written in Arabic, it combines the author's own ideas with those of his predecessors (notably Ibn Arabi and <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Ghazali</a>), all woven together in <i>Hafiz</i>, a Persian poetry form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jelias_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-jelias-116">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Levels_of_meaning">Levels of meaning</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quran_rzabasi1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2394" data-file-width="2898" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Quran_rzabasi1.JPG/220px-Quran_rzabasi1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Quran_rzabasi1.JPG/330px-Quran_rzabasi1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Quran_rzabasi1.JPG/440px-Quran_rzabasi1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quran_rzabasi1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
9th-century Quran in <a href="/wiki/Reza_Abbasi_Museum" title="Reza Abbasi Museum">Reza Abbasi Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike the Salafis and Zahiri, Shias and Sufis as well as some other <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Muslim philosophers</a> believe the meaning of the Quran is not restricted to the literal aspect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corbin_1993.2C_p.7_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corbin_1993.2C_p.7-117">[115]</a></sup> For them, it is an essential idea that the Quran also has inward aspects. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Corbin" title="Henry Corbin">Henry Corbin</a> narrates a <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i> that goes back to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The Quran possesses an external appearance and a hidden depth, an exoteric meaning and an esoteric meaning. This esoteric meaning in turn conceals an esoteric meaning (this depth possesses a depth, after the image of the celestial Spheres, which are enclosed within each other). So it goes on for seven esoteric meanings (seven depths of hidden depth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corbin_1993.2C_p.7_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Corbin_1993.2C_p.7-117">[115]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>According to this view, it has also become evident that the inner meaning of the Quran does not eradicate or invalidate its outward meaning. Rather, it is like the soul, which gives life to the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup> Corbin considers the Quran to play a part in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a>, because <a href="/wiki/Gnosiology" title="Gnosiology">gnosiology</a> itself goes hand in hand with <a href="/wiki/Prophet#Islam" title="Prophet">prophetology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Commentaries dealing with the <i><a href="/wiki/Zahir_(Islam)" title="Zahir (Islam)">zahir</a></i> (outward aspects) of the text are called <i>tafsir</i>, and hermeneutic and esoteric commentaries dealing with the <i><a href="/wiki/Batin_(Islam)" title="Batin (Islam)">batin</a></i> are called <i><a href="/wiki/Esoteric_interpretation_of_the_Quran" title="Esoteric interpretation of the Quran">ta'wil</a></i> ("interpretation" or "explanation"), which involves taking the text back to its beginning. Commentators with an esoteric slant believe that the ultimate meaning of the Quran is known only to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_3-13"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-3">[1]</a></sup> In contrast, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quranic_literalism" title="Quranic literalism">Quranic literalism</a>, followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salafis" title="Salafis">Salafis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zahiri" title="Zahiri">Zahiris</a>, is the belief that the Quran should only be taken at its apparent meaning.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reappropriation">Reappropriation</span></h3>
<p>Reappropriation is the name of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermeneutical" title="Hermeneutical">hermeneutical</a> style of some ex-Muslims who have converted to Christianity. Their style or reinterpretation is ad hoc and unsystematized and geared towards <a href="/wiki/Apologetics" title="Apologetics">apologetics</a>. This tradition of interpretation draws on the following practices: grammatical renegotiation, renegotiation of textual preference, retrieval, and concession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Translations">Translations</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quran_translations" title="Quran translations">Quran translations</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_translations_of_the_Quran" title="List of translations of the Quran">List of translations of the Quran</a></div>
<p>Translating the Quran has always been problematic and difficult. Many argue that the Quranic text cannot be reproduced in another language or form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-slate-121">[119]</a></sup> Furthermore, an Arabic word may have a <a href="/wiki/Polysemy" title="Polysemy">range of meanings</a> depending on the context, making an accurate translation even more difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, the Quran has been <a href="/wiki/Translation" title="Translation">translated</a> into most <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-5"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup> The first translator of the Quran was <a href="/wiki/Salman_the_Persian" title="Salman the Persian">Salman the Persian</a>, who translated surat <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Fatiha" title="Al-Fatiha">al-Fatiha</a></i> into <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> during the seventh century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> Another translation of the Quran was completed in 884 in Alwar (<a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, now <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>) by the orders of Abdullah bin Umar bin Abdul Aziz on the request of the Hindu Raja Mehruk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first fully attested complete translations of the Quran were done between the 10th and 12th centuries in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanid</a> king, <a href="/wiki/Mansur_I" title="Mansur I">Mansur I</a> (961–976), ordered a group of scholars from <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a> to translate the <a href="/wiki/Tafsir_al-Tabari" title="Tafsir al-Tabari">Tafsir al-Tabari</a>, originally in Arabic, into Persian. Later in the 11th century, one of the students of <a href="/wiki/Khwaja_Abdullah_Ansari" title="Khwaja Abdullah Ansari">Abu Mansur Abdullah al-Ansari</a> wrote a complete <i><a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">tafsir</a></i> of the Quran in Persian. In the 12th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Hafs_Omar_al-Nasafi" title="Abu Hafs Omar al-Nasafi">Najm al-Din Abu Hafs al-Nasafi</a> translated the Quran into Persian. The manuscripts of all three books have survived and have been published several times.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Islamic tradition also holds that translations were made for Emperor Negus of Abyssinia and Byzantine Emperor <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a>, as both <a href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_letters_to_the_Heads-of-State" title="Muhammad's letters to the Heads-of-State">received letters by Muhammad</a> containing verses from the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-6"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup> In early centuries, the permissibility of translations was not an issue, but whether one could use translations in prayer.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1936, translations in 102 languages were known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-7"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup> In 2010, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H%C3%BCrriyet_Daily_News_and_Economic_Review" title="Hürriyet Daily News and Economic Review">Hürriyet Daily News and Economic Review</a></i> reported that the Quran was presented in 112 languages at the 18th International Quran Exhibition in Tehran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_of_Ketton" title="Robert of Ketton">Robert of Ketton</a>'s 1143 translation of the Quran for <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Venerable" title="Peter the Venerable">Peter the Venerable</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_Mahumet_pseudoprophete" title="Lex Mahumet pseudoprophete">Lex Mahumet pseudoprophete</a></i>, was the first into a Western language (<a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Ross_(writer)" title="Alexander Ross (writer)">Alexander Ross</a> offered the first English version in 1649, from the French translation of <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Alcoran_de_Mahomet" title="L'Alcoran de Mahomet">L'Alcoran de Mahomet</a></i> (1647) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andre_du_Ryer" title="Andre du Ryer">Andre du Ryer</a>. In 1734, <a href="/wiki/George_Sale" title="George Sale">George Sale</a> produced the first scholarly translation of the Quran into English; another was produced by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bell_(Arabist)" title="Richard Bell (Arabist)">Richard Bell</a> in 1937, and yet another by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_John_Arberry" title="Arthur John Arberry">Arthur John Arberry</a> in 1955. All these translators were non-Muslims. There have been numerous translations by Muslims. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya_Muslim_Community" title="Ahmadiyya Muslim Community">Ahmadiyya Muslim Community</a> has published translations of the Quran in 50 different languages<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup> besides a <a href="/wiki/The_English_Commentary_of_the_Holy_Quran" title="The English Commentary of the Holy Quran">five-volume English commentary</a> and an <a href="/wiki/The_Holy_Quran_-_Arabic_Text_and_English_translation" title="The Holy Quran - Arabic Text and English translation">English translation of the Quran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>As with translations of the Bible, the English translators have sometimes favored archaic English words and constructions over their more modern or conventional equivalents; for example, two widely read translators, A. Yusuf Ali and M. Marmaduke Pickthall, use the plural and singular "ye" and "thou" instead of the more common "<a href="/wiki/You" title="You">you</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The oldest Gurumukhi translation of the Quran Sharif in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a> has been found in village <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Lande" title="Lande">lande</a> of <a href="/wiki/Moga,_Punjab" title="Moga, Punjab">Moga</a> district of Punjab which is around 115 years old, printed in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recitation">Recitation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rules_of_recitation">Rules of recitation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tajwid" title="Tajwid">Tajwid</a></div>
<p>The proper recitation of the Quran is the subject of a separate discipline named <i><a href="/wiki/Tajwid" title="Tajwid">tajwid</a></i> which determines in detail how the Quran should be recited, how each individual syllable is to be pronounced, the need to pay attention to the places where there should be a pause, to <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">elisions</a>, where the pronunciation should be long or short, where letters should be sounded together and where they should be kept separate, etc. It may be said that this discipline studies the laws and methods of the proper recitation of the Quran and covers three main areas: the proper pronunciation of <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a> (the articulation of the Quranic <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a>), the rules of pause in recitation and of resumption of recitation, and the musical and melodious features of recitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-8"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>In order to avoid incorrect pronunciation, reciters who are not native speakers of Arabic language try to follow a program of training in countries such as Egypt or Saudi Arabia. The recitations of a few Egyptian reciters were highly influential in the development of the art of recitation. Southeast Asia is well known for world-class recitation, evidenced in the popularity of the woman reciters such as <a href="/wiki/Maria_Ulfah" title="Maria Ulfah">Maria Ulfah</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-9"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are two types of recitation: <i>murattal</i> is at a slower pace, used for study and practice. <i><a href="/wiki/Mujawwad" title="Mujawwad">Mujawwad</a></i> refers to a slow recitation that deploys heightened technical artistry and melodic modulation, as in public performances by trained experts. It is directed to and dependent upon an audience for the <i>mujawwad</i> reciter seeks to involve the listeners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nelson_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-nelson-130">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variant_readings">Variant readings</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Qira%27at" title="Qira'at">Qira'at</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qur%27an_folio_11th_century_kufic.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="600" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Qur%27an_folio_11th_century_kufic.jpg/250px-Qur%27an_folio_11th_century_kufic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Qur%27an_folio_11th_century_kufic.jpg/375px-Qur%27an_folio_11th_century_kufic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Qur%27an_folio_11th_century_kufic.jpg/500px-Qur%27an_folio_11th_century_kufic.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qur%27an_folio_11th_century_kufic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Page of the Quran with vocalization marks</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Vocalization markers indicating specific vowel sounds were introduced into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a> by the end of the 9th century. The first Quranic manuscripts lacked these marks, therefore several recitations remain acceptable. The variation in readings of the text permitted by the nature of the defective vocalization led to an increase in the number of readings during the 10th century. The 10th-century Muslim scholar from <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_Ibn_Muj%C4%81hid" title="Abu Bakr Ibn Mujāhid">Ibn Mujāhid</a>, is famous for establishing seven acceptable textual readings of the Quran. He studied various readings and their trustworthiness and chose seven 8th-century readers from the cities of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kufa" title="Kufa">Kufa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>. Ibn Mujahid did not explain why he chose seven readers, rather than six or ten, but this may be related to a prophetic tradition (Muhammad's saying) reporting that the Quran had been revealed in seven "<i>ahruf</i>" (meaning seven letters or modes). Today, the most popular readings are those transmitted by <a href="/wiki/Hafs" title="Hafs">Ḥafṣ</a> (d.796) and <a href="/wiki/Warsh" title="Warsh">Warsh</a> (d. 812) which are according to two of Ibn Mujahid's reciters, <a href="/wiki/Aasim_ibn_Abi_al-Najud" title="Aasim ibn Abi al-Najud">Aasim ibn Abi al-Najud</a> (Kufa, d. 745) and <a href="/wiki/Nafi%E2%80%98_al-Madani" title="Nafi‘ al-Madani">Nafi‘ al-Madani</a> (Medina, d. 785), respectively. The influential standard Quran of Cairo (1924) uses an elaborate system of modified vowel-signs and a set of additional symbols for minute details and is based on ʻAsim's recitation, the 8th-century recitation of Kufa. This edition has become the standard for modern printings of the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-melchert2_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-melchert2-131">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>The variant readings of the Quran are one type of textual variant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_45-5"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> According to Melchert, the majority of disagreements have to do with vowels to supply, most of them in turn not conceivably reflecting dialectal differences and about one in eight disagreements has to do with whether to place dots above or below the line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melchert_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melchert-133">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nasser categorizes variant readings into various subtypes, including internal vowels, long vowels, <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">gemination</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaddah" title="Shaddah">shaddah</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">assimilation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alternation_(linguistics)" title="Alternation (linguistics)">alternation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasser_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasser-134">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Occasionally, an early Quran shows compatibility with a particular reading. A Syrian manuscript from the 8th century is shown to have been written according to the reading of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Amir_ad-Dimashqi" title="Ibn Amir ad-Dimashqi">Ibn Amir ad-Dimashqi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dutton_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-dutton-135">[133]</a></sup> Another study suggests that this manuscript bears the vocalization of <a href="/wiki/Homs" title="Homs">himsi</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rabb_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-rabb-136">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_and_printing">Writing and printing</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span></h3>
<p>Before printing was widely adopted in the 19th century, the Quran was transmitted in manuscripts made by <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">calligraphers</a> and copyists. The earliest manuscripts were written in <i><a href="/wiki/Hijazi_script" title="Hijazi script">Ḥijāzī</a></i>-type script. The <i>Hijazi</i> style manuscripts nevertheless confirm that transmission of the Quran in writing began at an early stage. Probably in the ninth century, scripts began to feature thicker strokes, which are traditionally known as <i><a href="/wiki/Kufic" title="Kufic">Kufic</a></i> scripts. Toward the end of the ninth century, new scripts began to appear in copies of the Quran and replace earlier scripts. The reason for discontinuation in the use of the earlier style was that it took too long to produce and the demand for copies was increasing. Copyists would therefore choose simpler writing styles. Beginning in the 11th century, the styles of writing employed were primarily the <i><a href="/wiki/Naskh_(script)" title="Naskh (script)">naskh</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Muhaqqaq" title="Muhaqqaq">muhaqqaq</a></i>, <i>rayḥānī</i> and, on rarer occasions, the <i>thuluth</i> script. <i>Naskh</i> was in very widespread use. In North Africa and Spain, the <i><a href="/wiki/Maghrebi_script" title="Maghrebi script">Maghribī</a></i> style was popular. More distinct is the <i>Bihari</i> script which was used solely in the north of India. <i><a href="/wiki/Nasta%CA%BFl%C4%ABq_script" title="Nastaʿlīq script">Nastaʻlīq</a></i> style was also rarely used in Persian world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_45-6"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the beginning, the Quran did not have vocalization markings. The system of vocalization, as we know it today, seems to have been introduced towards the end of the ninth century. Since it would have been too costly for most Muslims to purchase a manuscript, copies of the Quran were held in mosques in order to make them accessible to people. These copies frequently took the form of a series of 30 parts or <i>juzʼ</i>. In terms of productivity, the Ottoman copyists provide the best example. This was in response to widespread demand, unpopularity of printing methods and for aesthetic reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup></p>
