<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Printing">Printing</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">Wood-block printing</a> of extracts from the Quran is on record as early as the 10th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>Arabic <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> printing was ordered by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Julius_II" title="Pope Julius II">Pope Julius II</a> (r. 1503–1512) for distribution among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_Christians" title="Middle Eastern Christians">Middle Eastern Christians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> The first complete Quran printed with movable type was produced in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> in 1537/1538 for the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> market by <a href="/wiki/Paganino_Paganini" title="Paganino Paganini">Paganino Paganini</a> and Alessandro Paganini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> Two more editions include those published by the pastor <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Hinckelmann" title="Abraham Hinckelmann">Abraham Hinckelmann</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> in 1694 and by Italian priest <a href="/wiki/Louis_Maracci" title="Louis Maracci">Ludovico Maracci</a> in <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a> in 1698.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The latter edition included an accurate <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> translation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Printed copies of the Quran during this period met with strong opposition from <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">Muslim legal scholars</a>: printing anything in Arabic was prohibited in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_empire" title="Ottoman empire">Ottoman empire</a> between 1483 and 1726—initially, even on penalty of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup> The Ottoman ban on printing in Arabic script was lifted in 1726 for non-religious texts only upon the request of <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Muteferrika" title="Ibrahim Muteferrika">Ibrahim Muteferrika</a>, who printed his first book in 1729. Very few books, and no religious texts, were printed in the Ottoman Empire for another century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1786, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine the Great</a> of Russia, sponsored a printing press for "Tatar and Turkish orthography" in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, with one Mullah Osman Ismail responsible for producing the Arabic types. A Quran was printed with this press in 1787, reprinted in 1790 and 1793 in Saint Petersburg, and in 1803 in <a href="/wiki/Kazan_Governorate" title="Kazan Governorate">Kazan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup> The first edition printed in Iran appeared in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qajar_Iran" title="Qajar Iran">Tehran</a> (1828) and the first Ottoman edition was finally printed in 1877, during the <a href="/wiki/First_Constitutional_Era" title="First Constitutional Era">First Constitutional Era</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gustav_Leberecht_Fl%C3%BCgel" title="Gustav Leberecht Flügel">Gustav Flügel</a> published an edition of the Quran in 1834 in <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a>, which remained authoritative for close to a century, until Cairo's <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_University" title="Al-Azhar University">Al-Azhar University</a> published an edition of the Quran in 1924. This edition was the result of a long preparation as it standardized Quranic orthography and remains the basis of later editions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_45-7"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-45">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_other_literature">Relationship with other literature</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Bible">The Bible</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_narratives_and_the_Quran" title="Biblical narratives and the Quran">Biblical narratives and the Quran</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tawrat" title="Tawrat">Tawrat</a></div>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">It is He Who sent down to thee (step by step), in truth, the Book, confirming what went before it; and He sent down the Law (of Moses) and the Gospel (of Jesus) before this, as a guide to mankind, and He sent down the criterion (of judgment between right and wrong).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup></td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>— <strong class="selflink">Quran</strong> <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/3:3" rel="nofollow">3:3</a> <sup>(<a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Yusuf_Ali" title="Abdullah Yusuf Ali">Yusuf Ali</a>)</sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Quran speaks well<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of the relationship it has with former books (the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gospels" title="Gospels">Gospels</a>) and attributes their similarities to their unique origin and saying all of them have been revealed by the one God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Sahih al-Bukhari</a>, the Quran was recited among <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levantines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraqis</a>, and discussed by Christians and Jews, before it was standardized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (December 2016)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (December 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Its language was <a href="/wiki/The_Syro-Aramaic_Reading_of_the_Koran" title="The Syro-Aramaic Reading of the Koran">similar</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac language</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Quran recounts stories of many of the people and events recounted in <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> sacred books (<a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>) and devotional literature (<a href="/wiki/Apocrypha" title="Apocrypha">Apocrypha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a>), although it differs in many details. <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enoch_(ancestor_of_Noah)" title="Enoch (ancestor of Noah)">Enoch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hud_(prophet)" title="Hud (prophet)">Eber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shelah_(son_of_Judah)" title="Shelah (son of Judah)">Shelah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lot_(biblical_figure)" title="Lot (biblical figure)">Lot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ishmael" title="Ishmael">Ishmael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac" title="Isaac">Isaac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacob" title="Jacob">Jacob</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_(patriarch)" title="Joseph (patriarch)">Joseph</a>, <a href="/wiki/Job_(biblical_figure)" title="Job (biblical figure)">Job</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jethro_(Bible)" title="Jethro (Bible)">Jethro</a>, <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">David</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Elisha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jonah" title="Jonah">Jonah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aaron" title="Aaron">Aaron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zechariah_(priest)" title="Zechariah (priest)">Zechariah</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> are mentioned in the Quran as prophets of God (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" title="Prophets of Islam">Prophets of Islam</a>). In fact, <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses</a> is mentioned more in the Quran than any other individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keeler_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keeler-149">[147]</a></sup> Jesus is mentioned more often in the Quran than Muhammad, while <a href="/wiki/Mary_in_Islam" title="Mary in Islam">Mary</a> is mentioned in the Quran more than the New Testament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships">Relationships</span></h3>
<p>Some non-Muslim groups such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baha%27i" title="Baha'i">Baha'is</a> and <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a> view the Quran as holy. <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalists</a> may also seek inspiration from the Quran. The Quran has been noted to have certain narratives similarities to the <a href="/wiki/Diatessaron" title="Diatessaron">Diatessaron</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protoevangelium_of_James" title="Protoevangelium of James">Protoevangelium of James</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infancy_Gospel_of_Thomas" title="Infancy Gospel of Thomas">Infancy Gospel of Thomas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Pseudo-Matthew" title="Gospel of Pseudo-Matthew">Gospel of Pseudo-Matthew</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_Infancy_Gospel" title="Arabic Infancy Gospel">Arabic Infancy Gospel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> One scholar has suggested that the Diatessaron, as a <a href="/wiki/Gospel_harmony" title="Gospel harmony">gospel harmony</a>, may have led to the conception that the Christian Gospel is one text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_writing">Arab writing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Large_Koran.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="966" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Large_Koran.jpg/150px-Large_Koran.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Large_Koran.jpg/225px-Large_Koran.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Large_Koran.jpg/300px-Large_Koran.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Large_Koran.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Page from a Quran ('Umar-i Aqta'). <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid dynasty</a>, circa 1400. Opaque <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watercolor" title="Watercolor">watercolor</a>, ink and gold on paper Muqaqqaq script. 170 × 109 cm (66 <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>15</sup>⁄<sub>16</sub></span> × 42 <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>15</sup>⁄<sub>16</sub></span> in). Historical region: <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the Quran, and the general rise of Islam, the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic alphabet</a> developed rapidly into an art form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_49-10"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wadad Kadi, Professor of Near Eastern Languages and Civilizations at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>, and Mustansir Mir, Professor of Islamic studies at <a href="/wiki/Youngstown_State_University" title="Youngstown State University">Youngstown State University</a>, state:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Although Arabic, as a language and a literary tradition, was quite well developed by the time of Muhammad's prophetic activity, it was only after the emergence of Islam, with its founding scripture in Arabic, that the language reached its utmost capacity of expression, and the literature its highest point of complexity and sophistication. Indeed, it probably is no exaggeration to say that the Quran was one of the most conspicuous forces in the making of classical and post-classical Arabic literature.</p>
<p>The main areas in which the Quran exerted noticeable influence on Arabic literature are diction and themes; other areas are related to the literary aspects of the Quran particularly oaths (q.v.), metaphors, motifs and symbols. As far as diction is concerned, one could say that Quranic words, idioms and expressions, especially "loaded" and formulaic phrases, appear in practically all genres of literature and in such abundance that it is simply impossible to compile a full record of them. For not only did the Quran create an entirely new linguistic corpus to express its message, it also endowed old, pre-Islamic words with new meanings and it is these meanings that took root in the language and subsequently in the literature...</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Quran" title="Criticism of the Quran">Criticism of the Quran</a></div>
<p>The Qur'an's statements on the creation of the universe and earth, the origins of human life, biology, earth sciences and so on have been criticized by scientists as containing fallacies, being unscientific, and likely to be contradicted by evolving scientific theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup> Several scholars have said that it lacks clarity despite calling itself a clear book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup></p>

class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> especially in British English <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɒr/ 'or' in 'moral'">ɒr</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɑː/ 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Arabic_pronunciation-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Arabic_pronunciation_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Arabic pronunciation can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonemic_transcription" title="Phonemic transcription">transcribed phonemically</a> as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/al.qurˈʔaːn/</span>. The actual pronunciation in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Arabic" title="Literary Arabic">Literary Arabic</a> varies regionally. The first vowel varies from <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">o</a>]</span> to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">ʊ</a>]</span> to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">u</a>]</span>, while the second vowel varies from <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Near-open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open front unrounded vowel">æ</a>]</span> to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">a</a>]</span> to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ</a>]</span>. For example, the pronunciation in Egypt is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[qorˤˈʔɑːn]</span> and in Central East Arabia <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[qʊrˈʔæːn]</span>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

"God's Apostle replied, 'Sometimes it is (revealed) like the ringing of a bell, this form of Inspiration is the hardest of all and then this state passes off after I have grasped what is inspired. Sometimes the Angel comes in the form of a man and talks to me and I grasp whatever he says.' ʻAisha added: Verily I saw the Prophet being inspired Divinely on a very cold day and noticed the Sweat dropping from his forehead (as the Inspiration was over)."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-blomm-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-blomm_96-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The final process of collection and codification of the Quran text was guided by one over-arching principle: God's words must not in any way be distorted or sullied by human intervention. For this reason, no serious attempt, apparently, was made to edit the numerous revelations, organize them into thematic units, or present them in chronological order... This has given rise in the past to a great deal of criticism by European and American scholars of Islam, who find the Quran disorganized, repetitive and very difficult to read." <i>Approaches to the Asian Classics</i>, Irene Blomm, William Theodore De Bary, Columbia University Press, 1990, p. 65</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-pepys-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-pepys_97-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Samuel Pepys: "One feels it difficult to see how any mortal ever could consider this Quran as a Book written in Heaven, too good for the Earth; as a well-written book, or indeed as a book at all; and not a bewildered rhapsody; written, so far as writing goes, as badly as almost any book ever was!" <a class="external free" href="http://maxwellinstitute.byu.edu/display.php?table=review&amp;id=21" rel="nofollow">http://maxwellinstitute.byu.edu/display.php?table=review&amp;id=21</a></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWatson1968">Watson 1968</a>, p. 435; <a href="#CITEREFClogg1979">Clogg 1979</a>, p. 67</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"the major Ottoman printing houses published a combined total of only 142 books in more than a century of printing between 1727 and 1838. When taken in conjunction with the fact that only a minuscule number of copies of each book were printed, this statistic demonstrates that the introduction of the printing press did not transform Ottoman cultural life until the emergence of vibrant print media in the middle of the nineteenth century" Şükrü Hanioğlu, "A Brief History of the Late Ottoman Empire", Princeton University Press (2010), cited after Suresh Emre, <a class="external text" href="https://sureshemre.wordpress.com/2014/01/18/on-the-late-adoption-of-the-printing-press-in-the-ottoman-empire/" rel="nofollow">On the late adoption of the printing press in the Ottoman Empire</a> (2014).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"at imperial expense, a 'Tatar and Turkish Typography' was established in St. Petersburg; a domestic scholar, Mullah Osman Ismail, was responsible for the manufacture of the types. One of the first products of this printing house was the Qur'ān. Through the doctor and writer, Johann Georg v. Zimmermann (d. 1795), who was befriended by Catherine II, a copy of the publication arrived in the Göttingen University library. Its director, the philologist Christian Gottlob Heyne (d. 1812), presented the work immediately in the <i>Göttingische Anzeigen von gelehrten Sachen</i> (28 July 1788); therein he pointed especially to the beauty of the Arabic types. To the Arabic text marginal glosses have been added that consist predominantly of reading variants. The imprint was reproduced unchanged in 1790 and 1793 in St. Petersburg (cf. Schnurrer, Bibliotheca arabica, no. 384); later, after the transfer of the printing house to Kazan, editions appeared in different formats and with varying presentation (Dorn, <i>Chronologisches Verzeichnis</i>, 371)." <i>Encyclopaedia of the Qurʼān: P-Sh</i> ed. Jane Dammen McAuliffe, Brill, 2004, p. 251. For the 1803 Kazan edition: Chauvin, V.C. Bib. des ouvrages arabes, vol. X, 95; Schnurrer, C.F. von. Bibliotheca Arabica, 385. Original held by Bayerische Staatsbibliothek – Munich, Germany, Shelfmark BSB A.or.554.</span></li>
